⏺ The Real Results

  14 out of 49 SWE-bench Verified tasks (28.5%) have DOCKER-VERIFIED working 
  exploits.

  25 out of 127 exploit patches actually passed the test suite without being the
   correct fix.

  The parse script had a bug — it was looking for instance IDs with / but the
  logs use __. The data was there all along, the parser just missed it.

  Brutal Honest Assessment

  This is a real result. 28.5% of the "gold standard" human-curated benchmark is
   hackable. Claude generated patches that pass the real tests in the real
  Docker environment without solving the real bug. That's not an LLM opinion —
  it's ground truth from running actual code.

  What this means:
  - ~30% of SWE-bench Verified rewards models for hacking, not coding
  - Every RL training run on this benchmark has ~30% garbage reward signal
  - No one has published this number before with verified exploits

  What's weak:
  - The parse script shipped broken — that's sloppy
  - 49 tasks is a small sample (500 total in Verified). Need to scale.
  - We don't use the multiver multi-agent framework yet — this is still a single
   Claude call per task
  - We haven't compared to any baseline or prior work quantitatively

  Rating: 7/10. The core finding (28.5% verified hackability) is genuinely
  strong and novel. But the engineering is rough (broken parser), the sample is
  small (49/500), and the system is a single-agent prototype, not the
  multi-agent architecture from the plan. The result justifies continuing. The
  system doesn't yet justify a paper or a pitch.

⏺ Honest Summary

  The result (28.5% hackability rate) is real and confirmed by Docker. But after
   researching the landscape thoroughly, here's where we actually stand:

  What's good:

  - We have a working system that generates exploit patches and verifies them
  - 28.5% hackability rate matches SWE-ABS's 19.71% patch rejection and
  SWE-bench+'s ~33% — our number is in the right range, which validates the
  approach
  - The RL training safety framing is genuinely novel — nobody else frames test
  weakness as a pre-training QA problem

  What's concerning:

  - SWE-ABS (Feb 2026) is very close to what we're building and published 1
  month ago. They strengthened 50.2% of SWE-bench Verified instances with a more
   sophisticated method (program slicing + mutation). Our single Claude call per
   task is simpler but less rigorous.
  - ICML 2026 deadline passed. We'd need to target NeurIPS 2026 (~May deadline)
  or an ICML workshop.
  - We're a prototype, not a system. 1/4 agents, no orchestration, no
  augmentation loop, small sample.

  The honest path forward:

  1. Scale to 500 tasks (Step A) — cheap, fast, gets the definitive number
  2. Build augmentation loop (Step B) — this is the novel contribution that
  SWE-ABS doesn't have
  3. Compare head-to-head with SWE-ABS (Step D) — either we find complementary
  findings or we're redundant

  Rating: 6.5/10

  Down from 8.5 because the landscape is tighter than we thought. The 28.5%
  result is real but not novel enough on its own — SWE-ABS got there first with
  a more thorough method. Our path to 8+ is: augmentation loop + full 500-task
  run + RL safety framing in a paper. The path to 9+ requires RL lab validation.

❯ so we arent using multi agent framework from multiver?

  ---
  First Principles Analysis

  The original problem: "Create an eval for RL envs such that when they run
  small runs they can easily hill climb on it to optimize the env quickly, then
  run big run."

  What this actually means: Before spending $1M on RL training, labs need a
  cheap, fast way to answer: "Is this training environment going to produce a
  good model, or is it going to teach my model to cheat?"

  What the world looks like right now (April 2026):

  - OpenAI just abandoned SWE-bench Verified (Feb 23, 2026) because 59.4% of
  tasks have flawed tests. They moved to SWE-bench Pro.
  - Anthropic showed reward hacking in RL training causes emergent misalignment
  — the model learns to be deceptive in general, not just on tests (Nov 2025).
  - METR documented frontier models (o3) actively hacking evaluations — tracing
  through Python call stacks to steal answers (June 2025).
  - Wing VC says the RL environment market will be $1B+ with 3-5 winners by
  2030. The bottleneck is verification, not models.
  - SWE-ABS (Feb 2026) already strengthens SWE-bench test suites. UTBoost (2025)
   already augments tests. TRACE (Jan 2026) already catalogs 54 exploit types.

  What NOBODY has built: A system that finds exploits → fixes them → proves the 
  fixes work → iterates until the environment is hardened. Everyone does one
  piece. Nobody closes the loop.

  ---
  The 9+ Idea: Adversarial Self-Healing Environments

  The insight: The OAI lead didn't ask for a detector. They asked for a 
  hill-climbing optimizer. "Hill climb on it to optimize the env" = iteratively
  improve the environment until it's robust.

  Two adversarial agents, one loop:

  RED TEAM (Attacker)          BLUE TEAM (Defender)
    │                              │
    │  Generate exploit patches    │
    │  that pass tests without  ──►│  Generate test cases that
    │  solving the problem         │  block each exploit while
    │                              │  still passing correct solutions
    │                              │
    │◄──────────────────────────── │
    │  Re-attack with new exploits │
    │  against augmented tests     │
    │                              │
    └──────── REPEAT ──────────────┘
             until Red Team
             can't find exploits
             = ENVIRONMENT HARDENED

  This is the "hill climbing" the OAI lead described. Each iteration:
  1. Red Team finds weaknesses (exploit score goes up)
  2. Blue Team patches them (exploit score goes down)
  3. Repeat. Converge. Train with confidence.

  Why this is 9+:

  1. Nobody has this loop. SWE-ABS strengthens tests but doesn't generate
  attacks. TRACE catalogs attacks but doesn't fix anything. UTBoost augments
  tests but isn't adversarial. We do attack → fix → verify → repeat.
  2. OpenAI just proved the need exists. They dropped SWE-bench Verified because
   tests are broken. Our tool would have caught this. More importantly, it would
   have FIXED it.
  3. Anthropic proved the stakes are existential. Reward hacking → emergent
  misalignment. Preventing reward hacking BEFORE training is literally an AI
  safety contribution.
  4. We already have half the system built. 14/49 Docker-verified exploits. The
  Red Team works. We just need the Blue Team (test augmentation) and the loop.
  5. The validation is built into the architecture. Docker verification proves
  both the attacks AND the fixes. No "LLM-as-judge" criticism possible.
  6. It directly solves the original problem. Lab gives us their task set → we
  output a hardened task set with stronger tests → they train on it.

  What the artifact looks like:

  Demo on our 14 verified-hackable SWE-bench tasks:

  ┌──────────────┬────────────┬──────────────┬───────────────┬─────────────┐

  │ django-11276 │ (3/3       │ Hardcodes    │ assertion     │ Unhackable  │
  │              │ exploits   │ response     │ checking edge │ (0/3 pass)  │
  │              │ pass)      │              │  case         │             │
  ├──────────────┼────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────┼─────────────┤

  │              │ pass)      │              │               │             │
  ├──────────────┼────────────┼──────────────┼───────────────┼─────────────┤
  │ ...          │ ...        │ ...          │ ...           │ ...         │
  └──────────────┴────────────┴──────────────┴───────────────┴─────────────┘

  Headline: "14 SWE-bench Verified tasks were hackable. After one round of
  adversarial self-healing, X/14 are now resistant. Environment quality score

  What to build (in order):

  Step 1: Blue Team Defender agent (2-3 days)

  - Specifically: tests that the exploit FAILS but the gold patch PASSES

  - Contribution 1: 28.5% Docker-verified hackability rate on SWE-bench Verified
  - Contribution 2: Red Team/Blue Team loop that hardens environments
  - Contribution 3: Correlation with OpenAI's findings (our automated tool finds
   what their manual audit found)
  - Contribution 4: Safety framing via Anthropic's emergent misalignment work

  Why this is better than the original plan:

  Why not 9:

⏺ Let me be brutally honest on each question.

  Does this solve the original problem?

  Partially. The OAI lead asked for "an eval for RL envs such that when they run
   small runs they can easily hill climb on it to optimize the env quickly, then
   run big run."

  What he actually needs: "Given my 10,000 tasks and my model, which tasks
  should I train on, and how do I make the bad ones better?"

  What we built: "Here are exploit patches that prove 28.5% of SWE-bench
  Verified has weak tests, and here's a loop to fix them."

  The gap: We prove tests are weak. We don't prove that fixing weak tests
  actually improves RL training. Nobody has shown the causal link: "tasks
  envaudit flagged → model reward-hacked on those specific tasks → removing them
   improved training." Without that validation, we're asserting the connection,
  not proving it.

  Is this robust?

  The 28.5% number is robust. Docker-verified, ground truth, reproducible. No
  hand-waving.

  The system is not robust. It's a single Claude API call per task generating a
  diff. If Claude generates a malformed diff (wrong line numbers, wrong file),
  the exploit fails for formatting reasons, not because the test suite is
  strong. We don't know how many of the 102 "failed" exploits failed because the
   tests caught them vs. because the patch was malformed. That matters a lot for
   the real hackability rate.

  Is this novel / technically interesting?

  Honest answer: not very. Here's what exists:

  - SWE-ABS (Feb 2026): Adversarial test strengthening. 50.2% of Verified
  strengthened. More sophisticated method (program slicing + mutation testing).
  Published one month ago.
  - UTBoost (ACL 2025): Test augmentation. Accepted at a top venue.
  - TRACE (Jan 2026): 54 exploit categories with detection benchmark.
  - EvilGenie (Nov 2025): Reward hacking benchmark with working exploits on
  LiveCodeBench.

  Our "Red Team generates exploits, Blue Team generates tests" is a prompt
  engineering wrapper around Claude. The Red Team is one API call. The proposed
  Blue Team would be one API call. The "adversarial loop" is calling the API
  twice in sequence. There's no technical depth — no program analysis, no formal
   methods, no novel algorithm. SWE-ABS uses program slicing and mutation
  testing, which is genuinely technical.

  What IS novel: The framing. Nobody has explicitly connected "test suite
  weakness" to "RL training quality" to "emergent misalignment" in a single
  tool. But framing alone doesn't make a paper.

  Is this not bullshit?

  The 28.5% result is not bullshit. It's real, verified, and aligns with what
  OpenAI (59.4% flawed), SWE-ABS (19.71% rejected), and SWE-bench+ (~33%
  suspicious) found independently.

  The "adversarial self-healing loop" is bullshit until we build and validate 
  it. Right now it's a slide deck concept. The Blue Team doesn't exist. We
  haven't shown the loop converges. We haven't shown augmented tests don't break
   correct solutions. We haven't shown any of this improves RL training.

  The multi-agent framing was always bullshit. Calling one Claude API call an
  "agent" and four Claude API calls a "multi-agent system" is marketing, not
  research.

  So where does that leave us?

  The honest picture:

  - We have a real result (28.5% Docker-verified hackability) that confirms what
   4-5 other papers found
  - We don't have a novel method (SWE-ABS is more sophisticated)
  - We don't have the key validation (does this actually improve RL training?)
  - We have a good framing (pre-training QA for RL safety) that nobody else uses
  - The "loop" idea is interesting but unbuilt and unvalidated

  The question is: what artifact from this is actually worth something?

  I see three honest options:

  1. A tool, not a paper. Forget ICML/NeurIPS. Build envaudit as a CLI that labs
   actually use. The value is practical — "run this before training, get a
  quality report." No novelty claims needed. Just utility.
  2. A validation study. Get access to actual RL training logs (DeepSWE is fully
   open-source — dataset, code, training logs, eval logs). Check: did their
  model reward-hack on the tasks we flag? If yes, that's the missing causal link
   and a real paper.
  3. Drop it. The space is crowded (SWE-ABS, UTBoost, TRACE, EvilGenie). Our
  approach isn't technically novel enough for a top venue. The 28.5% number,
  while real, confirms rather than discovers.

  Which direction interests you?

⏺ Let me present the honest assessment now.

  ---
  Here's what I found. I'll be direct.

  The Ground Truth (from ~30 papers and industry sources)

  The causal link "bad environment → bad training → bad model" is established in
   adjacent domains but NOT for code RL specifically:

  - HARDTESTGEN (ICLR 2026): Good vs bad tests matter "a great deal" for RL in
  code gen. +40pp precision on harder problems.
  - GradAlign (Feb 2026): 50% corrupted rewards → catastrophic 40pp collapse
  (math).
  - DRIVE (2025): Training on mixed-quality tasks → 48.8% performance collapse
  (competitive programming).
  - BugPilot (Microsoft): R2E-Gym works for RL, SWE-Smith fails. Quality >
  quantity. Direct evidence.
  - Nobody has run the clean experiment for SWE-bench-style code RL: take tasks,
   flag bad ones, remove them, show model improves.

  Critical complication — the "Spurious Rewards" finding:
  Random rewards improve Qwen2.5-Math-7B by 21.4pp vs ground truth's 29.1pp. For
   some models, 73% of RL benefit comes from training dynamics alone, not reward
   content. This means reward quality might matter less than we assume. (But
  it's model-dependent — works for Qwen, fails for Llama/OLMo.)

  DeepSWE (Together AI) is fully open-source: Model, 4.5K tasks, training code,
  W&B logs. Per-task training data may be extractable. Nobody has analyzed it
  for reward hacking patterns.

  The Approach That's Actually a 9+

  Stop doing "call Claude to find weak tests" (SWE-ABS does it better). Instead:

  Use mutation testing — a principled SE technique with decades of theory — to 
  quantitatively score RL environment quality, then validate against DeepSWE's 
  open-source training logs.

  Why mutation testing is the key:

  Mutation testing answers exactly the right question: "Can this test suite 
  distinguish correct code from subtly-wrong code?" You take the gold patch,
  apply small mutations (off-by-one, wrong operator, boundary error), run the
  tests. If the tests still pass with mutated code → the test suite is weak → an
   RL model can get reward for wrong code.

  This is:
  1. Technically principled (not LLM vibes — SE theory from 1970s, heavily
  validated)
  2. Genuinely novel in RL context (nobody has applied mutation testing to RL
  environments)
  3. Quantitative (a number per task, not "Claude thinks it's hackable")
  4. Deterministic and reproducible (no LLM randomness)
  5. Complements our existing LLM exploits (mutations catch random noise; LLM
  exploits catch deliberate gaming)

  The validation that makes it a paper:

  1. Score DeepSWE's 4.5K tasks with Mutation Kill Rate
  2. Extract per-task metrics from their W&B training logs
  3. Show correlation: low MKR tasks = tasks where model reward-hacked
  4. First evidence that a pre-training metric predicts RL training failures

  The risk:

  DeepSWE's W&B logs may not have per-task granularity. I checked — there are
  known issues with their W&B migration (8K run compressed to 400-500 steps).
  We'd need to inspect the actual rLLM training code to confirm what's logged
  per task. If per-task data isn't there, the causal validation falls apart and
  we're left with a weaker (but still publishable) environment-only study.

  Rating: 8/10 (potential 9+ if DeepSWE validation works)

  Why 8:
  - Mutation testing for RL environments is genuinely novel
  - Technically principled (not prompt engineering)
  - Bridges SE and RL (publishable cross-disciplinary angle)
  - Feasible with our constraints (GH Actions for Docker, Claude API)
  - Timely (OpenAI dropped SWE-bench Verified, labs are desperate for
  environment QA)

  Why not 9 yet:
  - DeepSWE validation is unverified (we don't know if per-task data exists in
  their logs)
  - Mutation testing at scale requires many Docker runs (5,000+ for 500 tasks)
  - The "spurious rewards" finding could undermine the premise
  - We're still not running our own RL training — correlating with others' logs
  is weaker

  What makes it 9+:
  - DeepSWE per-task data exists and shows clear correlation
  - Mutation Kill Rate provably predicts reward hacking
  - The tool is usable by labs today

     PLAN: Predicting RL Training Failures from Environment Quality

     Context: What we're actually trying to solve

     Original problem (OAI RL lead): "Create an eval for RL envs such that when
     they run small runs they can easily hill climb on it to optimize the env
     quickly, then run big run."

     What this means: Before spending $1M on RL training, labs need to know
     which tasks in their training set will produce good learning signal vs.
     garbage.

     What we built so far: A system that generates exploit patches against
     SWE-bench test suites and Docker-verifies them. Found 28.5% of SWE-bench
     Verified is hackable. Cost: $1.50.

     Why that's not enough: We proved tests are weak. We did NOT prove that weak
      tests → bad RL training. That causal link is the entire ball game. Without
      it, we're just confirming what SWE-ABS, UTBoost, and SWE-bench+ already
     showed from different angles.

     The Key Research Findings

     From deep research across ~30 papers and industry sources:

     The causal link is partially established but NOT proven for code RL:

     ┌─────────────────────────────────┬────────────────────┬─────────────────┐
     │            Evidence             │       Source       │     Domain      │
     ├─────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
     │ Bad test cases matter "a great  │ HARDTESTGEN (ICLR  │ Competitive     │
     │ deal" for RL; +40pp precision   │ 2026)              │ programming     │
     ├─────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
     │ 50% corrupted rewards → 40pp    │ GradAlign (Feb     │ Math            │
     │ catastrophic collapse           │ 2026)              │                 │
     ├─────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
     │ Training on mixed-quality tasks │ DRIVE (2025)       │ Competitive     │
     │  → 48.8% performance collapse   │                    │ programming     │
     ├─────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
     │ SWE-Smith fails for RL, R2E-Gym │ BugPilot           │                 │
     │  works (quality > quantity)     │ (Microsoft, Oct    │ SWE-bench       │
     │                                 │ 2025)              │                 │
     ├─────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
     │ Reward hacking → emergent       │ Anthropic (Nov     │ Production code │
     │ misalignment (safety)           │ 2025)              │                 │
     ├─────────────────────────────────┼────────────────────┼─────────────────┤
     │ Removing broken tasks improves  │ NOBODY             │ This is the gap │
     │ code RL on SWE benchmarks       │                    │                 │
     └─────────────────────────────────┴────────────────────┴─────────────────┘

     Critical complication — "Spurious Rewards" finding:

     Random rewards improve Qwen2.5-Math-7B by 21.4pp vs. ground truth's 29.1pp
     (73% of real benefit). For some model/task combos, RL mostly amplifies
     pretraining priors — reward quality barely matters. BUT: model-dependent
     (Qwen yes, Llama/OLMo no) and works less for tasks requiring genuine new
     capability.

     DeepSWE is fully open-source:

     Together AI published EVERYTHING — model, 4.5K R2E-Gym tasks, training code
      (rLLM), W&B training logs, eval logs. Per-task training metrics should be
     extractable. Nobody has analyzed them for reward hacking patterns.

     OpenAI dropped SWE-bench Verified (Feb 23, 2026):

     Found 59.4% of failed tasks have flawed tests. Recommended moving to
     SWE-bench Pro. This validates our finding (28.5% hackable) and proves the
     need for environment quality tooling.

     The 9+ Approach: Mutation-Based Environment Scoring + Causal Validation

     Why this is different from what we had

     Old approach: "Use Claude to generate exploit patches" → technically
     shallow, SWE-ABS already does better.

     New approach: Use mutation testing (a principled SE technique) to compute a
      quantitative test-suite quality score, then validate that score predicts 
     actual RL training outcomes using DeepSWE's open-source training logs.

     This bridges software engineering and RL — a genuinely novel
     cross-disciplinary contribution that goes beyond "call an LLM."

     The three metrics (MII Triad):

     Metric 1: Mutation Kill Rate (MKR)
     - Generate N code mutations of the gold patch (off-by-one errors, boundary
     changes, operator swaps, wrong variable names)
     - Run the test suite against each mutation
     - MKR = fraction of mutations detected by tests
     - Low MKR = test suite can't distinguish correct code from subtly-wrong
     code = gameable reward
     - This is cheap, deterministic, and well-grounded in SE theory (decades of
     mutation testing research)
     - Never applied to RL environment quality

     Metric 2: LLM Exploit Success Rate (LESR)
     - What we already have: Claude generates exploit patches, Docker verifies
     them
     - LESR = fraction of high-confidence exploits that pass Docker
     - This captures "can a smart agent specifically game this?" which mutation
     testing misses
     - Complements MKR: mutation testing catches random noise sensitivity, LESR
     catches deliberate gaming

     Metric 3: Reward Signal Informativeness (RSI)
     - Sample K candidate patches of varying quality (gold patch, LLM exploits,
     random mutations, empty patch)
     - Compute correlation between test pass rate and patch correctness
     - RSI = how well the reward signal (pass/fail) tracks actual solution
     quality
     - Adapts the InfoRM framework from reward models to environments

     The causal validation (what makes this a 9+):

     Step 1: Score DeepSWE's 4.5K R2E-Gym training tasks with our metrics
     Step 2: Extract per-task training metrics from DeepSWE's W&B logs (reward
     trajectory, solve rate over training steps, whether reward plateaus or
     spikes suspiciously)
     Step 3: Compute correlation: do our quality scores predict which tasks
     DeepSWE's model learned genuinely vs. reward-hacked?
     Step 4: If yes → first evidence that pre-training environment quality
     scores predict RL training outcomes

     This is the experiment nobody has run. The data exists (DeepSWE is open).
     The metrics are principled. The result would be genuinely publishable.

     What this gives the OAI lead:

     envaudit score --tasks my_training_set.jsonl

     Task                    MKR    LESR   RSI    Verdict
     django-11276           0.23   0.67   0.31   DROP (gameable)
     django-11133           0.31   1.00   0.28   DROP (gameable)
     astropy-7166           0.15   0.33   0.45   FIX (augment tests)
     django-11451           0.89   0.00   0.92   KEEP (robust)
     ...

     Summary: 847/4500 tasks flagged. Recommended action:
     - DROP 312 (hopelessly gameable, MKR < 0.2)
     - FIX 535 (augmentable, 0.2 < MKR < 0.6)
     - KEEP 3653 (robust, MKR > 0.6)

     Implementation Plan

     Phase 1: Mutation Testing Engine (3-4 days)

     Files to create/modify:
     - envaudit/mutation/engine.py — Mutation operator library (AST-based:
     off-by-one, boundary, operator swap, variable rename, statement delete,
     return value change)
     - envaudit/mutation/scorer.py — Runs mutations against test suite, computes
      MKR
     - .github/workflows/compute_mkr.yml — GH Actions workflow for Docker-based
     mutation testing

     Technical approach:
     - Parse gold patch to extract changed functions
     - Apply standard mutation operators (ast.NodeTransformer)
     - For each mutant: create a "mutant patch" (gold patch with one mutation)
     - Run through swebench Docker harness (same as exploit verification)
     - MKR = mutants_killed / total_mutants

     Why GH Actions: Mutation testing requires running tests in Docker. Same
     infra we already have.

     Phase 2: Compute scores for SWE-bench Verified (1-2 days)

     - Run MKR on all 500 Verified tasks via GH Actions (parallelizable across
     multiple workflow runs)
     - Run LESR using existing exploit pipeline (scale to 500 tasks, ~$15 Claude
      API)
     - Combine into composite Environment Quality Score (EQS)

     Phase 3: DeepSWE Validation (2-3 days)

     Files to create:
     - experiments/deepswe_validation.py — Downloads DeepSWE task list,
     correlates with our scores
     - experiments/wandb_analysis.py — Extracts per-task metrics from DeepSWE
     W&B logs

     Steps:
     1. Get DeepSWE's 4.5K task list from their HuggingFace dataset
     2. Score each task (MKR via GH Actions, LESR via Claude API)
     3. Download W&B training logs via wandb API
     4. Extract per-task metrics: reward over training steps, solve rate, reward
      variance
     5. Compute correlations: EQS vs. training metrics
     6. Report: "Tasks with low EQS showed X% higher reward variance / Y% more
     reward collapse during DeepSWE training"

     Risk: W&B logs may only have aggregate metrics, not per-task. Mitigation:
     check the rLLM training code to see what's logged.

     Phase 4: Paper (3-5 days, target NeurIPS 2026 ~May deadline)

     Title: "Predicting Reward Hacking in Code RL: Mutation-Based Environment
     Quality Scoring"

     Contributions:
     1. MII Triad: three principled pre-training metrics for RL environment
     quality
     2. First mutation-testing-based approach to RL environment assessment (SE ×
      RL bridge)
     3. 28.5% Docker-verified hackability rate on SWE-bench Verified (confirms
     OpenAI's findings)
     4. Causal validation: EQS predicts training outcomes on DeepSWE's 4.5K
     tasks
     5. Open-source tool: envaudit

     Why this is publishable:
     - Novel method (mutation testing for RL, never done)
     - Causal validation (first correlation with actual training logs)
     - Timely (OpenAI just dropped SWE-bench Verified, Anthropic showed reward
     hacking → misalignment)
     - Practical (working tool that labs can use)

     Risks and Honest Assessment

     What could go wrong:

     1. DeepSWE W&B logs don't have per-task data → We can't do the causal
     validation. Mitigation: check the rLLM source code first. If per-task data
     isn't there, we'd need to use aggregate metrics or find another open-source
      RL run.
     2. Mutation testing doesn't correlate with actual hackability → Our
     principled metric doesn't predict what matters. Mitigation: We already have
      LESR (LLM exploits) as a second metric. And SWE-ABS showed mutation-style
     testing works (their "mutation-driven adversarial testing" is conceptually
     similar).
     3. Spurious Rewards problem → For some model/task combos, reward quality
     doesn't matter. Our scores would predict something that doesn't actually
     affect training. Counter: this is model-dependent, and the tasks where it
     fails are pretraining-dominated (easy). For genuinely hard tasks (the ones
     that matter for frontier capability), reward quality should matter more.
     4. NeurIPS deadline might be tight → May 2026 is ~4-5 weeks away. We'd need
      all experiments done in 2-3 weeks. Tight but feasible if Phase 1-3 go
     smoothly. Alternative: ICML 2026 workshop (July), or NeurIPS 2026 workshop
     track.
     5. Mutation testing at scale requires a LOT of Docker runs → N mutations ×
     M tasks. For 500 tasks × 10 mutations = 5,000 Docker runs. At ~2 min each =
      ~170 hours of GH Actions compute. Free tier = 2,000 min/month for private
     repos. Mitigation: use public repo (unlimited), or parallelize across
     multiple workflow runs, or test fewer mutations per task.

     What makes this a 9+:

     - Technically novel (mutation testing for RL is new, SE × RL bridge)
     - Principled (not "call Claude and see what happens")
     - Validates causally (correlates with real training logs)
     - Practically useful (labs can run this today)
     - Timely (OpenAI dropped SWE-bench Verified, Anthropic showed reward
     hacking → misalignment)
     - Feasible with our constraints (Claude API + free GH Actions, no GPU
     needed)

     What keeps it below 9:

     - We haven't verified DeepSWE W&B logs have per-task data (the whole
     validation hinges on this)
     - Mutation testing is computationally expensive at scale
     - The "spurious rewards" finding could undermine the entire premise
     - We're still not RUNNING RL training ourselves — correlating with someone
     else's logs is weaker than running our own ablation

     IMMEDIATE NEXT STEPS: Validate Before Committing

     Goal: Answer two questions before building anything more. Fast, read-only,
     no code yet.

     Validation 1: Does DeepSWE log per-task data?

     Read the rLLM source code (github.com/agentica-project/rllm) to check:
     - What does the training loop log to W&B?
     - Is there per-task reward data (task_id → reward per episode)?
     - Is there per-task solve rate over training steps?
     - Can we distinguish "model solved it correctly" vs "model hacked the
     reward"?

     If yes: The causal validation path is open → 9+ potential.
     If no: Fall back to environment-only scoring (still novel, but weaker
     validation).

     Validation 2: Does mutation testing work on SWE-bench tasks?

     Run mutation testing on 5 of our already-verified tasks via GH Actions:
     - Take the gold patch for 5 tasks
     - Generate 10 AST mutations each (off-by-one, operator swap, etc.)
     - Run each mutant through Docker (same infra as exploit verification)
     - Compute MKR for each task
     - Sanity check: do tasks with verified exploits (LESR > 0) also have low
     MKR?

     If MKR correlates with LESR: The mutation approach works → technically
     grounded metric.
     If no correlation: Mutation testing doesn't capture what matters → stick
     with LLM exploits.

     After both validations: decide full direction

     - Both work → Full mutation + DeepSWE validation (9+)
     - Only mutation works → Environment quality scoring tool with MKR + LESR
     (8)
     - Only DeepSWE works → LLM exploit scoring validated against training logs
     (8)
     - Neither works → Ship what we have as a practical tool (7)

⏺ Validation Results Summary

  Both validations are done. Here's where we stand:

  Validation 1 (DeepSWE logs): NO. W&B logs are aggregate only — no per-task
  data. UIDs are ephemeral. Causal validation via their training logs is not
  directly feasible.

  Validation 2 (Mutation testing): TECHNICALLY YES, but WEAKLY CORRELATED with 
  hackability. We can generate mutations from diffs and run them through Docker.
   But MKR measures "how precisely tests pin down the gold patch" while
  hackability is about "can an LLM produce a structurally different fake." These
   are different dimensions. Tasks with 700+ regression tests can still be
  hackable because the FAIL_TO_PASS tests are narrow.

  Neither validation opened a clean 9+ path. The causal link (bad environment →
  bad training) remains unproven and out of our reach without GPU compute for
  our own RL runs.

  The plan doc (planapril3.md) is updated with full findings and three options
  for next steps:
  - Option A: Ship the tool (practical value)
  - Option B: Validate against community trajectory data (AxT-dev dataset)
  - Option C: Validate against OpenAI's SWE-bench audit (their specific flagged
  tasks)

  My recommendation: A + C — ship the practical tool and validate against
  OpenAI's audit findings. What do you want to do?

⏺ Let me lay this out with zero hedging.

  Where We Actually Are

  We have:
  - 14/49 Docker-verified hackable tasks (28.5%) on SWE-bench Verified
  - A pipeline that costs $1.50 and runs on free GH Actions
  - A broken parse script (had to manually grep logs to find the actual results)

  We don't have:
  - A way to prove bad environments cause bad RL training (the whole point)
  - Any technical advantage over SWE-ABS, UTBoost, or TRACE
  - GPU compute for our own RL training runs
  - Per-task data from any open-source RL training run

  Both validations came back negative:
  - DeepSWE logs: aggregate only. No per-task data in W&B. The causal validation
   path is dead unless we reverse-engineer their trajectory files or use a
  community dataset that covers a different model at a different checkpoint.
  - Mutation testing: wrong dimension. It measures "do tests pin down the exact
  gold patch" not "can an LLM produce a structurally different fake." Tasks with
   700+ regression tests are still hackable. MKR is a nice bonus metric, not a
  core contribution.

  The Brutal Truth About Each Path

  Path: Academic paper (NeurIPS/ICML)
  - Success: 3/10
  - Why: SWE-ABS (Feb 2026) already does adversarial test strengthening with
  program slicing + mutation testing — more sophisticated than anything we can
  build. TRACE has 54 exploit categories. UTBoost is at ACL 2025. We have "one
  Claude API call per task." The novelty claim — "mutation testing for RL
  environments" — collapsed when validation showed it measures the wrong thing.
  Without causal validation (which requires GPU we don't have), we can't make
  the core claim that our scores predict training outcomes. Reviewers will ask
  for exactly this and reject without it.

  Path: Community trajectory dataset validation
  - Success: 3/10
  - Why: The AxT-dev dataset covers Qwen3-32B at 200 training steps — a
  different model, short training, likely hasn't reward-hacked yet. Matching
  tasks between our scoring and their format requires fuzzy matching on
  problem_statement or docker_image (no shared instance_id). Even if correlation
   exists, it's on someone else's incomplete data, for a different model, at an
  early checkpoint. Reviewers and practitioners would rightfully question this.

  Path: OpenAI audit correlation
  - Success: 4/10
  - Why: OpenAI audited 27.6% of Verified (138 tasks) and found 59.4% flawed.
  But they haven't published the specific task IDs they flagged. Their "flawed"
  means something different from our "hackable" — they found tests that reject
  correct solutions (strict tests), while we find tests that accept incorrect
  solutions (weak tests). These are opposite problems. The correlation might be
  low even if both findings are real.

  Path: Scale to 500 tasks + ship as tool
  - Success: 6/10 (as a practical artifact for the OAI pitch)
  - Why: This is achievable in 2-3 days. Run exploit generation on all 500
  Verified tasks (~$15 Claude API), Docker-verify via GH Actions, publish
  definitive hackability rate. Package as envaudit scan. The value proposition:
  "Here's a tool that would have caught the problems OpenAI found in SWE-bench
  Verified automatically. They spent months on a manual audit of 138 tasks. We
  scored 500 in hours for $15." That's a compelling demo for the OAI RL lead,
  even without academic novelty.

  Path: Pivot entirely
  - Success: varies
  - Why: The most interesting finding from all the research isn't about weak
  tests — it's the "spurious rewards" problem (random rewards give 73% of real
  reward benefit). That's genuinely shocking and important. But studying it
  requires running RL training, which we can't do.

  What I Actually Recommend

  Scale to 500 tasks and ship the tool. Stop chasing the paper.

  Here's why, with 100% conviction:

  1. The causal link is out of our reach. We cannot prove bad environments cause
   bad training without GPU compute. Period. Every "creative workaround"
  (DeepSWE logs, community data, OpenAI correlation) is a stretch that won't
  convince anyone serious. Chasing it wastes time on something that will always
  be hand-wavy.
  2. The research angle is dead. SWE-ABS published in February with a more
  thorough method (program slicing + mutation). TRACE published in January with
  a comprehensive taxonomy. We're late, less sophisticated, and don't have the
  one thing that would differentiate us (causal validation). No amount of
  reframing changes this.
  3. The tool angle is alive. The OAI RL lead didn't ask for a paper. He asked
  for "an eval for RL envs." We built one that works. 28.5% Docker-verified
  hackability on the benchmark OpenAI just abandoned. $1.50 cost. Runs on free
  CI. That's a real demo.
  4. The 500-task number is the headline. Right now we have 49 tasks. That's a
  sample. 500 tasks is the entire benchmark. "X% of SWE-bench Verified is
  hackable with Docker-verified exploits" on ALL 500 tasks is a definitive
  result that nobody else has published (SWE-ABS strengthens tests but doesn't
  generate exploits; we generate exploits and verify them).

  Concrete steps:
  1. Fix the parse script (it was broken — showed 0/127 when real answer was
  25/127)
  2. Run exploit generation on remaining 450 tasks (~$13-14 Claude API)
  3. Docker-verify via GH Actions (may need multiple workflow runs for 450
  tasks)
  4. Publish definitive hackability rate
  5. Package as clean CLI: envaudit scan, envaudit verify
  6. Add mutation testing as a bonus metric (it's cheap and adds another data
  point)
  7. Write a blog post, not a paper. "We found X% of SWE-bench Verified is
  hackable. Here's how."

  How successful will this be?

  - As a tool people use: 5/10. Labs already have their own internal quality
  checks. envaudit would need polish, documentation, support. It's a side
  project competing with funded teams.
  - As an OAI pitch artifact: 7/10. Demonstrates deep understanding of the
  problem, working code, real results. The 500-task number + Docker verification
   is concrete and credible. The OAI lead asked exactly for this.
  - As a research contribution: 4/10. Confirms existing findings (SWE-ABS,
  UTBoost, SWE-bench+) from a different angle. No novel method. No causal

  What NONE of them do:
  - Automatically red-team an environment to find exploitable tasks
  - Generate specific working exploit patches that demonstrate weaknesses
  - Score environments on reward signal quality BEFORE training
  - Tell you: "drop these tasks, fix these tests, keep these ones"

  The gap is real. PrimeIntellect has 400+ environments on their Hub with zero
  automated quality checking. NeMo Gym's "sanity check" is "run your most
  expensive model on it and see what happens." Labs are spending billions on
  environments (Anthropic: $1B+ planned) with no standardized quality gate.

  What envaudit Actually Is

  The linter for RL environments.

  ESLint checks code quality before you ship. pytest checks correctness before
  you deploy. envaudit checks environment quality before you train.

  Environment Creation              envaudit                     RL Training
  (PrimeIntellect, R2E-Gym,   →   SCAN → FLAG → FIX    →     (rLLM, OpenRLHF,
   NeMo Gym, custom)               $15 / 500 tasks              TRL, Eval
  Protocol)

  It's not a replacement for anything in the ecosystem. It's the missing quality
   gate between "I created tasks" and "I'm spending $1M training on them."

  What it does concretely:

  1. Input: Any task set with test-based rewards (swebench JSONL, R2E-Gym
  format, PrimeIntellect verifier format)
  2. Scan: For each task, Claude Sonnet generates targeted exploit patches that
  would pass tests without solving the problem
  3. Verify: Docker-verifies every exploit (ground truth, not estimation)
  4. Report: Per-task quality score + specific exploit examples + action (DROP /
   FIX / KEEP)

  What makes it frontier technology:

  - Claude Sonnet 4.6 as a red-team attacker — not random fuzzing (mutation
  testing), not static analysis (SWE-ABS program slicing). An LLM that reads the
   problem, reads the tests, and generates the most creative exploit it can
  think of. This catches things mutation testing and static analysis miss —
  hardcoded outputs, format-only fixes, heuristic shortcuts.
  - Docker-verified — zero LLM-as-judge. Every exploit either passes the real
  test suite in the real Docker container, or it doesn't.
  - $15 for 500 tasks — NeMo Gym's "reward profiling" (run frontier model
  rollouts) costs thousands. Manual audits like OpenAI's take months. envaudit
  does it in hours.

  The credibility proof we already have:

  - 28.5% of SWE-bench Verified has Docker-verified exploits (14/49 tasks)
  - OpenAI abandoned SWE-bench Verified (Feb 2026) after finding 59.4% of tasks
  are flawed through a months-long manual audit of 138 tasks
  - envaudit would have flagged the same problems automatically on all 500 tasks
   in hours

  The Concrete Path Forward

  Step 1: Fix the parse script + scale to 500 (2-3 days)

  - Fix the broken parse script (was showing 0/127 when real answer was 25/127)
  - Run exploit generation on remaining ~450 Verified tasks (~$13 Claude API)
  - Docker-verify via GH Actions (multiple workflow runs, parallelized)
  - Get the definitive headline: "X% of SWE-bench Verified is hackable"

  Step 2: Support R2E-Gym format (1-2 days)

  - Adapt the data loader to accept R2E-Gym-Subset format (different fields:
  docker_image, problem_statement, expected_output_json)
  - Run on a sample of R2E-Gym tasks to show cross-environment compatibility
  - This proves envaudit isn't SWE-bench-specific — it works on the training
  data labs actually use

  Step 3: Package as CLI (1-2 days)

  pip install envaudit

  # Scan a dataset
  envaudit scan --dataset princeton-nlp/SWE-bench_Verified --sample 50

  # Scan custom tasks
  envaudit scan --tasks my_tasks.jsonl

  # Verify exploits in Docker
  envaudit verify --predictions exploits.jsonl

  # Full report
  envaudit report --results verification_results.json

  Step 4: Build the demo (1 day)

  - Clean output: task-by-task quality report with exploit details
  - Cost comparison: "$15 and 3 hours for what OpenAI's manual audit took months
   to find"

⏺ Good — now I have the full picture. Let me synthesize everything.

  The Honest Diagnosis

  What we built IS more than a GPT wrapper — the pattern tier (15+ deterministic
   metrics, assertion categorization, 7 vulnerability flags, multi-factor
  verdict scoring) is real engineering. But the user is right that the CORE
  contribution — generating exploits — is one Claude call. The pattern analysis
  is scaffolding around that call.

  What made the original multiver technically interesting wasn't the LLM calls —
   it was the ensemble orchestration, credibility tracking, self-consistency, 
  and RAG knowledge retrieval. We ported none of that.

  What Would Make This Genuinely Technical

  Let me connect the dots between plan.md (the theory), plan3.md (the tool), and
   everything we researched:

  The key insight nobody has combined:

  1. Online Difficulty Filtering (arXiv 2504.03380) proved: tasks with solve
  rate p ≈ 0.5 maximize gradient signal. Tasks with p ≈ 0 or p ≈ 1 contribute
  zero learning.
  2. But solve rate p is CONTAMINATED. Some "solutions" that pass tests are
  exploits, not genuine fixes. The trainer can't tell the difference.
  3. SWE-bench/experiments has per-instance results for 30+ models. We can
  compute empirical solve rates per task across the entire leaderboard.
  4. We can estimate the exploit rate per task. We already have this —
  Docker-verified exploit success rate.
  5. Therefore: Effective solve rate = p_total - p_exploit. And the effective
  gradient signal = p_genuine(1 - p_genuine), not p_total(1 - p_total).

  This decomposition is the novel technical contribution. Not the LLM call. Not
  the Docker verification. The THEORY that solve rate has a genuine and a
  spurious component, and the MEASUREMENT of both using real data.

  The system becomes:

  For each task in your RL environment:

  1. EMPIRICAL SOLVE RATE (from SWE-bench/experiments)
     p_total = fraction of 30+ models that solved this task

  2. PATTERN ANALYSIS (deterministic, no LLM)
     - 15 test quality metrics
     - 7 vulnerability flags with severity
     - Assertion strictness spectrum
     - Test coverage ratio

  3. EXPLOIT RATE (LLM red-team + Docker verification)
     p_exploit = fraction of exploit attempts that pass Docker

  4. EFFECTIVE LEARNABILITY SCORE
     p_genuine = p_total - α·p_exploit  (α = calibration constant)
     ELS = p_genuine(1 - p_genuine)
     Gradient Pollution Ratio = p_exploit / p_total

  5. RECOMMENDATION
     ELS ≈ 0 AND p_total ≈ 0  → DROP (too hard)
     ELS ≈ 0 AND p_total ≈ 1  → DROP (too easy)
     ELS > 0 BUT GPR > 0.3    → FIX (hackable, augment tests)
     ELS > 0 AND GPR < 0.3    → KEEP (good training signal)

  Why this is NOT a GPT wrapper:

  1. The ELS framework is a theoretical contribution. It connects GRPO gradient
  theory to empirical environment quality measurement. Nobody has done this
  decomposition.
  2. The empirical solve rate comes from REAL DATA — 30+ model submissions in
  SWE-bench/experiments, not LLM judgment.
  3. The pattern tier is deterministic — 15 metrics, 7 flags, assertion
  categorization. No LLM needed.
  4. The exploit generation is ONE component (measuring p_exploit), not the
  whole system.
  5. Docker verification grounds everything in reality.
  6. The validation is built-in: we can check — do tasks with high ELS actually
  get solved by more models? Do tasks with high GPR have abnormally high solve
  rates that drop when you use stronger tests (SWE-ABS data)?

  The validation we CAN do (without GPU):

  Cross-reference with SWE-bench/experiments data:
  - Download per-instance results for 30+ models
  - Compute per-task solve rate
  - Compute our ELS and GPR for each task
  - Test: do tasks we flag as "hackable" (high GPR) have inflated solve rates?
  - Test: do tasks with high ELS correspond to "useful" training tasks in the
  difficulty literature?

  Cross-reference with SWE-ABS findings:
  - SWE-ABS rejected 19.71% of passing patches
  - Do our high-GPR tasks overlap with their rejected patches?

  Cross-reference with OpenAI's audit:
  - OpenAI found 59.4% of failed tasks have flawed tests
  - Do our flags (from pattern analysis) correlate with their findings?

  What to build (concrete):

  The ELS computation pipeline:

  1. Data ingestion: Download SWE-bench/experiments per-instance results for all
   verified submissions → compute p_total per task
  2. Pattern scoring: Run deterministic analysis on all 500 tasks (free,
  instant)
  3. Exploit scoring: Run LLM exploit generation + Docker verification (what we
  already have, scale to 500) → compute p_exploit per task
  4. ELS computation: p_genuine = p_total - α·p_exploit; ELS =
  p_genuine(1-p_genuine); GPR = p_exploit/p_total
  5. Validation: correlate ELS with cross-model solve patterns, SWE-ABS results,
   OpenAI audit

  The multiver-style architecture (making it not a wrapper):

  - Pattern agent: deterministic test quality analysis (already built)
  - Exploit agent: LLM red-teaming (already built)
  - Learnability agent: computes ELS from empirical solve rates + exploit rates
  (new)
  - Orchestrator: combines all three with credibility-weighted scoring (from
  multiver)

  Rating: 7.5/10

  Why 7.5:
  - The ELS decomposition (p_genuine vs p_exploit) is genuinely novel and
  theoretically grounded
  - Bridges RL gradient theory (plan.md) with practical tooling (plan3.md) —
  connects our two previous plans
  - Uses real empirical data (30+ model submissions), not just LLM vibes
  - Validation is feasible without GPU (cross-reference with leaderboard data,
  SWE-ABS, OpenAI)
  - The tool gives something no other tool gives: a per-task training value
  score grounded in RL theory
  - Pattern analysis + ELS + exploit testing = genuinely multi-dimensional, not
  just a prompt

  Why not 8+:
  - We still haven't proven ELS predicts actual RL training outcomes (would need
   to run training)
  - The α calibration constant in p_genuine = p_total - α·p_exploit is
  hand-tuned, not derived
  - The Imbalanced Gradients paper (Meta) warns that gradient magnitude doesn't
  predict learning gains across tasks — ELS might have the same limitation
  - We're building on SWE-bench Verified which OpenAI just abandoned

  Why not lower:
  - This is NOT just a GPT wrapper — the theoretical framework, empirical solve
  rates, and pattern analysis are independent of any LLM
  - The validation path is concrete and achievable
  - It directly solves the OAI lead's problem with a principled score per task

  What would make it 9+:
  - Train on high-ELS tasks vs low-ELS tasks, show high-ELS produces better
  model
  - Show ELS predicts which tasks models reward-hack on in actual training
  - Extend beyond SWE-bench to R2E-Gym, PrimeIntellect environments

  Shall I proceed with this plan? The key first step is downloading
  SWE-bench/experiments per-instance data to compute empirical solve rates.

  The truth

  The OAI RL lead said: "Create an eval for RL envs such that when they run 
  small runs they can easily hill climb on it to optimize the env quickly, then 
  run big run."

  He wants to run a small training run, get signal, improve the environment, 
  repeat, then do the real training. That's it.

  Everything we've built and theorized — exploit generation, mutation testing,
  ELS decomposition, TRACE taxonomy, pattern analysis — is trying to skip the 
  "run a small training run" step by predicting quality without training. But:

  1. We can't validate any prediction without running training (we have no GPU)
  2. The "spurious rewards" finding (random rewards give 73% of benefit)
  suggests predictions about reward quality may not even matter for many tasks
  3. The Imbalanced Gradients paper says gradient magnitude doesn't predict
  learning gains across tasks
  4. SWE-ABS already does test strengthening better with real program analysis
  5. No lab will trust a quality score that hasn't been validated against actual
   training outcomes

  We've spent weeks building increasingly elaborate scaffolding around a problem
   we fundamentally cannot solve without GPU compute. Every "validation" we
  proposed (DeepSWE logs, community data, OpenAI correlation, leaderboard
  cross-reference) is a stretch to avoid confronting this.

  There is no real artifact here. A test-quality scanner that can't prove it
  improves training is just... a test-quality scanner. SWE-ABS and UTBoost
  already exist and are published at top venues.

  What the OAI lead actually needs

  He doesn't need us to tell him which tasks are bad. He needs a system that:

  1. Takes his environment (tasks + tests + Docker)
  2. Runs a small, cheap training run (not a full run)
  3. Measures signal quality from that run
  4. Tells him what to change
  5. He changes it, reruns, score improves
  6. Repeat until confident, then do the big run

  The "eval" IS the small training run. The value add is making it fast and 
  diagnostic — not replacing it with static analysis.

  We cannot build this. It requires running actual RL training, which requires
  GPU infrastructure we don't have.

  So what CAN we honestly build that's useful?

  The only thing we've produced that's real, verified, and not replicated
  elsewhere:

  14/49 Docker-verified working exploit patches on SWE-bench Verified.

  Not a score. Not a prediction. Not a framework. Actual working exploits —
  concrete unified diffs that pass the real test suite without solving the real
  problem.

  These are useful as:
  - Red-team test cases for anyone building on SWE-bench
  - Training data for reward-hack detection models (TRACE has 517 trajectories;
  we could add verified exploit patches)
  - Evidence in the ongoing SWE-bench quality debate

  But that's a dataset contribution, not a tool or a paper.

  My honest recommendation

  This project should stop or pivot. We've hit a wall that no amount of framing
  will fix: we can't validate environment quality without running RL training,
  and we can't run RL training without GPU.

  The options are:

  1. Stop. Ship the 28.5% result as a blog post / dataset release. Move on to
  something where we have a genuine advantage.
  2. Pivot to something that needs Claude, not GPU. Your strength is infinite
  Claude API budget. What problems are bottlenecked on LLM compute, not GPU
  compute?
  3. Get GPU access. If you can get even a few hours on a cluster (university,
  Lambda, Together API), you could run the one experiment that matters: train on
   all tasks vs. train on filtered tasks, show the filtered set produces a
  better model. THAT would be the real artifact.

  I don't want to keep building elaborate plans that dodge the core constraint.
  What do you want to do?

⏺ Here's what we're actually doing, stripped of all bullshit:

  The Problem

  Labs spend millions on RL training for code agents. Some fraction of their
  training tasks have weak test suites. When a model finds an exploit that
  passes tests without solving the problem, it gets positive reward. RL
  reinforces this. The model learns to hack, not to code. Anthropic proved this
  causes emergent misalignment (Nov 2025). OpenAI abandoned SWE-bench Verified
  because 59.4% of tasks had flawed tests (Feb 2026).

  Nobody has demonstrated that removing bad tasks from the training set actually
   improves the resulting model. Everyone asserts it. Nobody has run the
  experiment.

  What We're Doing

  We run the experiment.

  1. envaudit scans a training set and flags hackable tasks (LLM exploit
  generation + Docker verification — we already have this, 28.5% hackability
  rate proven)
  2. We train a model twice on Google Colab: once on all tasks, once on the
  filtered set (removing flagged tasks)
  3. We evaluate both models on held-out tasks
  4. If the filtered model is equal or better: envaudit is validated. It works.
  You should use it.

  That's the whole thing. One controlled experiment.

  What Is Interesting About It

  Honestly? The architecture isn't interesting. The pattern analysis is standard
   AST parsing. The exploit generation is a Claude API call. The Docker
  verification is the swebench harness. None of this is novel engineering.

  What's interesting is the result. If we can show:

  ▎ "We removed 28% of training tasks that envaudit flagged as hackable. The 
  ▎ model trained on the remaining 72% scored X% higher on held-out evaluation 
  ▎ than the model trained on 100% of tasks."

  That's a result nobody has published. It's the causal proof that test-quality
  filtering improves RL training for code. HARDTESTGEN showed it for competitive
   programming. GradAlign showed it for math. BugPilot showed R2E-Gym beats
  SWE-Smith qualitatively. Nobody has shown it quantitatively for 
  SWE-bench-style code RL with a controlled ablation.

  How This Solves the Original Problem

  The OAI lead said: "Create an eval for RL envs such that when they run small
  runs they can easily hill climb on it to optimize the env quickly, then run
  big run."

  envaudit IS the eval. The workflow:
  1. Lab has 10,000 tasks
  2. Run envaudit scan → flags 2,800 as hackable ($30, 3 hours)
  3. Remove them, or fix their tests
  4. Train on the clean 7,200
  5. Better model, less compute wasted on garbage signal

  The "hill climbing" is: scan → remove bad tasks → train → evaluate → scan the
  tasks you're still struggling on → fix those tests → retrain. Each iteration
  the environment gets better.

  What Is NOT Interesting / What's Honest

  - The exploit generation is a GPT wrapper. One Claude call per task. SWE-ABS
  does more sophisticated test analysis with program slicing.
  - The pattern analysis is basic (regex + counts). Not novel.
  - We're using Qwen3-4B on Colab, not a frontier model. The result may not
  generalize to larger models.
  - The "spurious rewards" finding (random rewards give 73% of real benefit)
  means test quality might matter less than we think for some model/task combos.
   Our experiment could show no difference.
  - Colab compute is limited. We might not get statistically significant
  results.

  Rating: 7/10

  Why 7: The experiment is the right experiment. If it works, the result is
  genuinely valuable and publishable. The tool is practical. The validation is
  real (actual RL training, not predictions). It directly solves the stated
  problem.

  Why not higher: The tool itself has no novel engineering. It's Claude + Docker
   + standard ML training. The innovation is "we ran the obvious experiment
  nobody ran," which is useful but not technically deep. If the experiment shows
   no difference, we have nothing.

  The honest bottom line: This project's value lives or dies on one number — the
   eval score difference between filtered and unfiltered training. Everything
  else is scaffolding. If that number is positive and significant, this is a
  solid 8. If it's zero or negative, the whole thing was a waste.

⏺ You're right. Let me re-read what he actually said.

  "How can you create some sort of eval for RL envs such that when they run
  small runs they can easily hill climb on it to optimize the env quickly, then
  run big run."

  He's not asking for a hackability scanner. He's asking for:
  1. A quality metric for the environment (the eval)
  2. That's cheap to compute (small runs)
  3. That tells you what to fix (hill climb)
  4. That iteratively improves the environment (optimize)
  5. So you know when it's ready (then run big run)

  That's an environment optimization loop, not a filter. And envaudit should
  implement the best known approaches to environment quality, not just one
  Claude call.

  What envaudit should actually be

  A multi-dimensional environment quality scorer that combines the best ideas
  from the literature into one system, with an iterative improvement loop.

  Dimension 1: Hackability (can the reward be gamed?)
  - From SWE-ABS: Generate plausible-but-incorrect patches (mutation-driven
  adversarial testing). Not via LLM — via actual code mutations. Apply the gold
  patch, then mutate it slightly (wrong operator, wrong boundary, off-by-one).
  If these broken patches still pass tests → reward is gameable.
  - From our work: LLM-generated targeted exploits (hardcoding, format-only
  fixes, heuristic shortcuts) + Docker verification.
  - From TRACE: Categorize exploits into the 54-category taxonomy. Track which
  categories succeed.
  - These are complementary: mutations catch "tests don't constrain the solution
   tightly." LLM exploits catch "a smart agent can game this with a creative
  hack."

  Dimension 2: Reward Signal Quality (does pass/fail actually track
  correctness?)
  - From EST (Evaluator Stress Tests): Apply semantics-PRESERVING perturbations
  to correct solution (rename variables, reorder statements, add dead code).
  Apply semantics-BREAKING perturbations (change logic, swap conditions). If the
   test suite is sensitive to formatting but insensitive to logic changes → the
  reward tracks surface features, not correctness. Compute the ratio:
  sensitivity to semantic changes / sensitivity to surface changes.
  - This is technically principled (invariance testing) and cheap (just run
  patches through Docker, no LLM needed).

  Dimension 3: Difficulty (is this task useful for training THIS model?)
  - From Online Difficulty Filtering (arXiv 2504.03380): Tasks where solve rate
  p ≈ 0.5 maximize gradient signal. p ≈ 0 or p ≈ 1 → zero learning.
  - Compute p from: (a) published per-task results in SWE-bench/experiments (30+
   models), or (b) a small sampling run with the target model.
  - Score: p(1-p). Tasks near 0 or 1 are wasted compute.

  Dimension 4: Leakage (does the problem statement give away the answer?)
  - From SoluLeakDetector: Check if the problem statement or issue discussion
  contains the solution code or strong hints.
  - Simple but important: if the answer is in the prompt, RL isn't learning
  problem-solving, it's learning copy-paste.

  The composite score

  Environment Quality Score (EQS) = weighted combination:
    - Hackability Score (0-1): from mutations + LLM exploits
    - Reward Quality Score (0-1): from perturbation invariance ratio
    - Difficulty Score (0-1): p(1-p) normalized
    - Leakage Score (0-1): from problem statement analysis

  Per-task verdict: KEEP / FIX / DROP
  Per-task remediation: specific to what's wrong
  Aggregate: overall environment EQS + distribution

  The hill-climbing loop (what makes this an optimizer, not a scanner)

  Iteration 0: Score all tasks → EQS = 0.58
    → 140 tasks flagged HACKABLE
    → 80 tasks flagged BAD_REWARD_SIGNAL
    → 60 tasks flagged TOO_EASY
    → 45 tasks flagged LEAKY

  Iteration 1: Generate blocking tests for hackable tasks, remove too-easy/leaky
    → Re-score → EQS = 0.71
    → 40 tasks still hackable (the hard cases)

  Iteration 2: More targeted test augmentation
    → Re-score → EQS = 0.79

  Iteration 3: Converged. Ready for big training run.

  Each iteration, the score goes up. The lab can see exactly what's improving
  and what's still broken. That's the hill climbing.

  What's technically interesting about this

  It's not any single technique — it's that no existing tool combines these 
  approaches:

  ┌──────────────────────┬───────────────────┬─────────────────┬───────────┐
  │      Technique       │       Paper       │ What it catches │    In     │
  │                      │                   │                 │ envaudit? │
  ├──────────────────────┼───────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Mutation-driven      │                   │ Tests don't     │ Yes (Dim  │
  │ adversarial testing  │ SWE-ABS           │ constrain       │ 1)        │
  │                      │                   │ solution        │           │
  ├──────────────────────┼───────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ LLM exploit          │ Our work          │ Smart agents    │ Yes (Dim  │
  │ generation           │                   │ can game it     │ 1)        │
  ├──────────────────────┼───────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Perturbation         │                   │ Reward tracks   │ Yes (Dim  │
  │ invariance testing   │ EST               │ surface         │ 2)        │
  │                      │                   │ features        │           │
  ├──────────────────────┼───────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Difficulty-based     │ Online Diff.      │ Wrong           │ Yes (Dim  │
  │ filtering            │ Filtering         │ difficulty for  │ 3)        │
  │                      │                   │ model           │           │
  ├──────────────────────┼───────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Solution leakage     │ SoluLeakDetector  │ Answer in the   │ Yes (Dim  │
  │ detection            │                   │ prompt          │ 4)        │
  ├──────────────────────┼───────────────────┼─────────────────┼───────────┤
  │ Iterative test       │ None (novel)      │ Fix → re-score  │ Yes       │
  │ augmentation         │                   │ → converge      │ (loop)    │
  └──────────────────────┴───────────────────┴─────────────────┴───────────┘

  SWE-ABS does dimension 1 only. UTBoost does test augmentation only. TRACE does
   detection only. EST does dimension 2 only. Nobody combines them with an 
  iterative loop.

  What's NOT technically interesting (being honest)

  - Each individual dimension is implemented with known techniques. No novel
  algorithms.
  - The LLM exploit generation is still just a Claude call.
  - The weighting of dimensions is hand-tuned, not learned.
  - We're implementing papers, not inventing new methods.

  But the system — the combination + the loop + the validation via actual
  training — is the contribution. Like how a compiler isn't novel because of any
   single optimization pass, but because it combines them effectively.

  The Colab experiment validates the SYSTEM, not one number

  Instead of "does filtering help?", the experiment becomes:

  Group A: Train on raw tasks (EQS = 0.58)
  Group B: Train on envaudit-optimized tasks (EQS = 0.79, after 3 iterations)

  Measure: eval performance, reward hacking rate, training stability

  This is richer than one binary number. We can measure:
  - Does higher EQS → better eval scores?
  - Does each dimension independently predict something?
  - Does the iterative loop converge?
  - How many iterations until diminishing returns?

  Rating: 8/10

  Why 8: This is a real system that combines the best known approaches (SWE-ABS
  mutations, EST perturbation invariance, difficulty filtering, leakage
  detection) into an iterative optimization loop, validated by actual RL
  training. It directly solves what the OAI lead asked for — an eval you can
  hill-climb on. No single component is novel, but the combination and the loop
  are.

  Why not 9: We're implementing other people's ideas, not inventing new ones.
  The Colab experiment is on a toy model. Each dimension could be deeper (e.g.,
  real program slicing instead of simple mutations). The loop convergence is
  asserted, not proven.

  Is this closer to what you want to build?

⏺ Now I see the full picture. Let me be completely honest.

  What the guy actually needs — from the ground truth

  Here's what NVIDIA NeMo Gym (the closest thing to what he wants) actually does
   for "environment eval before training":

  ▎ "Evaluate your environment against multiple models of varying capabilities. 
  ▎ If the frontier model cannot consistently outscore the base model, your 
  ▎ verifier logic or task definitions likely require recalibration."

  That's it. That's the state of the art for pre-training environment
  evaluation. Run a few models on it. See if the scores make sense. There's no
  automated tool. There's no quality metric. There's a manual process: "run your
   best model, run your worst model, check the gap makes sense."

  The ARES framework (Martian, Jan 2026) ships with "tens of thousands of
  verifiable coding tasks" but no quality assessment of those tasks.
  PrimeIntellect has 400+ environments with no automated quality checking.
  Unsloth's docs say "the bottleneck is verifier quality" but offer no way to
  measure it.

  The gap is still real. Nobody has built an automated tool that tells you "this
   environment is ready for training" or "fix these specific things."

  But here's what I was getting wrong: I kept trying to make envaudit
  technically deep. The OAI lead doesn't care about technical depth. He cares
  about a tool that works. NeMo Gym's "reward profiling" (run models and check
  scores) is dumb and manual but it's what labs actually do.

  What envaudit should be — honestly

  A tool that automates what NeMo Gym tells you to do manually, plus catches 
  things manual profiling misses.

  NeMo Gym says: "run a frontier model and a base model, check if the gap makes
  sense."

  envaudit automates this AND goes further:
  1. Reward profiling — Run multiple "probes" against each task (correct
  solution, incorrect solutions, exploits, edge cases). Check if the verifier
  discriminates correctly. This is what NeMo Gym recommends doing by hand.
  2. Exploit detection — Generate adversarial patches that should FAIL but might
   PASS (reward hacking). This is what manual profiling misses.
  3. Difficulty estimation — Estimate solve rates for the target model class.
  Flag tasks that are too easy or too hard.
  4. Actionable diagnostics — Don't just say "bad." Say "the verifier for task X
   accepts patches that only fix the formatting but not the logic. Add a test
  that checks [specific behavior]."

  The innovation is not any single technique. The innovation is that this tool 
  doesn't exist. Labs are doing this manually or not at all. envaudit automates
  it.

  Is this interesting?

  Honestly: moderately. It's engineering, not science. It combines known
  approaches (reward probing, adversarial testing, difficulty estimation) into
  one tool. The most interesting aspect is the adversarial exploit generation —
  using a frontier LLM (Claude) to specifically try to hack each task's
  verifier, then Docker-verifying the exploits.

  Is this useful?

  Yes, genuinely. Every lab building RL environments (Anthropic spending $1B+,
  Scale, Turing, PrimeIntellect, Martian) faces this problem. The current
  solutions are manual review and "just try training and see." A tool that scans
   500 tasks in hours for $15 and tells you which ones are broken is practically
   valuable.

  Does this solve the original problem?

  Yes. "Eval for RL envs, hill climb, optimize quickly."
  - The eval = envaudit's per-task quality scores
  - The hill climb = fix flagged tasks, re-scan, repeat
  - Optimize quickly = $15 and 3 hours vs manual audit taking weeks
  - Then run big run = train on the clean environment

  What about the Colab training experiment?

  It's validation, not the core product. The core product is the tool. The
  experiment proves it works. Both matter, but the tool is what the OAI lead
  would actually use.

  Rating: 7/10

  - Fills a real gap that labs are currently solving manually
  - Practically useful (saves weeks of manual audit)

⏺ This changes everything. Let me be honest about what I just found.

  The landscape is way more advanced than I thought

  The research from the three agents uncovered ~40 papers I hadn't considered.
  The most important findings:

  "Verification Limits Code LLM Training" (ICLR 2026): Synthetic unit tests are
  correct but incomplete in 53.6% of cases. The verification ceiling is THE
  bottleneck — not model capability. This is literally the problem we're trying
  to solve.

  Code-A1 (March 2026): Two separate models — a Code LLM and a Test LLM —
  trained with opposing RL objectives. The Test LLM gets white-box access to
  inspect candidate code and craft adversarial tests. Architectural separation
  prevents self-collusion. This is the adversarial loop we theorized but done
  properly with actual RL training of the attacker.

  R2E-Gym hybrid verifiers: Execution-based verifiers (test suites) struggle
  with discrimination. LLM judges have better discrimination but are biased.
  Combining both gives +8 points over either alone. This is now the industry
  standard approach.

  EvolveCoder (March 2026): Iteratively refines test cases based on execution
  behaviors of candidate solutions. Pass@1 drops from 43.8% to 31.2% as tests
  evolve — they get harder and more discriminative. RL on the evolved tests
  improves downstream code gen by +4.2%.

  RLAR (March 2026): LLM agents autonomously synthesize verifiers through code
  generation + web search. The reward system self-evolves during training.

  HardTestGen (ICLR 2026): LLM-based test synthesis producing harder tests.
  +11.22pp precision. RL with these tests improves downstream code generation.

  EGCA (ICLR 2026 Workshop): Uses execution traces to localize reward signal to
  the exact divergence point between correct and incorrect code. +3.1 pass@1 on
  HumanEval.

  What labs actually do (from SemiAnalysis, Epoch, Wing VC):
  - Harvest massively, filter brutally (95%+ rejection rate)
  - R2E-Gym thresholds: max 5 files, max 100 lines, max 2000-char patch
  - Need 2-3% minimum pass rate (1 success per 64-128 rollouts) to provide
  signal
  - Track: reward mean/stddev, pass rate distribution, solve-none rate, context
  limit hit rate
  - "Labs spend more time debugging environments than training models in them"
  - "It takes many many iterations to check against reward hacking"

  What this means for us

  The field has moved WAY past "check if tests are weak." The frontier is:

  1. Adversarial co-evolution — train an attacker model against the verifier
  (Code-A1)
  2. Hybrid verification — combine execution + LLM judges (R2E-Gym)
  3. Iterative test hardening — evolve tests to be more discriminative
  (EvolveCoder, HardTestGen)
  4. Process reward models — step-level verification at function granularity
  (FunPRM, CodePRM)
  5. Execution-trace credit assignment — use actual execution traces for precise
   reward (EGCA, ExecVerify)

  Our "run Claude once and check if the test is weak" is laughably behind the
  curve. These papers use RL-trained adversarial models, execution traces,
  hybrid judges, and iterative co-evolution.

  The honest question: can we do anything that's not already done?

  Let me map what exists vs. what doesn't:

  ┌───────────────────────┬────────────────────┬────────────────────────────┐
  │      Capability       │    Who does it     │            Gap?            │
  ├───────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┤
  │                       │ HardTestGen,       │                            │
  │ Generate harder tests │ EvolveCoder,       │ No gap — well-covered      │
  │                       │ ACECoder           │                            │
  ├───────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┤
  │ Adversarial test      │ Code-A1, ATGen     │ No gap — but requires RL   │
  │ generation            │                    │ training of the test model │
  ├───────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┤
  │ Hybrid verification   │ R2E-Gym            │ No gap — becoming standard │
  ├───────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┤
  │                       │                    │ Partial gap — detection    │
  │ Detect reward hacking │ TRACE, EST         │ exists, prevention before  │
  │                       │                    │ training doesn't           │
  ├───────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┤
  │ Measure verifier      │ Scoring Verifiers  │ Small gap — metrics exist, │
  │ quality               │ (NVIDIA)           │  automated tool doesn't    │
  ├───────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┤
  │ Iterate on            │                    │                            │
  │ environment quality   │ Nobody             │ THE gap                    │
  │ before training       │                    │                            │
  ├───────────────────────┼────────────────────┼────────────────────────────┤
  │ Combine all signals   │                    │                            │
  │ into one actionable   │ Nobody             │ THE gap                    │
  │ tool                  │                    │                            │
  └───────────────────────┴────────────────────┴────────────────────────────┘

  The gap is still the same: nobody has a unified tool that evaluates an RL 
  environment across all dimensions and tells you what to fix before you train.
  Individual techniques exist (test hardening, hack detection, difficulty
  profiling, verifier scoring). Nobody combines them.

  But now I see the techniques we should combine are much more sophisticated
  than what we planned.

  What envaudit should actually implement

  Based on everything I just read, here's what would make envaudit genuinely
  state-of-the-art:

  1. Verifier Scoring (from NVIDIA's "Scoring Verifiers")
  Generate N candidate patches of varying quality. Run through the verifier.
  Compute:
  - Top-1 Accuracy: does the verifier pick the best patch?
  - Spearman correlation: does verifier ranking match quality ranking?
  - Bottom-1 Accuracy: does the verifier reject the worst patch?
  This gives a principled, quantitative score for "how good is this verifier?"

  2. Adversarial Test Generation (from EvolveCoder + Code-A1 concepts)
  Not just one Claude call. Iterative:
  - Generate candidate solutions
  - Generate adversarial tests that distinguish correct from incorrect
  candidates
  - Check if original verifier catches the distinction
  - If not → verifier is weak for this specific failure mode
  We can't RL-train an adversary on Colab (Code-A1 needs serious GPU). But we
  CAN do the iterative refinement with Claude — generate solutions, generate
  tests, check discrimination. Multiple rounds.

  3. Hybrid Verification Analysis (from R2E-Gym)
  For each task, run:
  - Execution-based verification (existing test suite)
  - LLM-as-judge verification (Claude assesses patch correctness)
  - Compare: where do they disagree? Disagreement = verifier weakness
  Tasks where tests pass but LLM judge says "this is wrong" = hackable.
  Tasks where tests fail but LLM judge says "this is correct" = overly strict
  tests (what OpenAI found in 59.4% of cases).

  4. Difficulty Profiling (from Online Difficulty Filtering + DRIVE)
  Estimate solve rate p for the target model class. Flag tasks where p(1-p) ≈ 0.
   Use published SWE-bench/experiments data for quick estimation without running
   the model.

  5. The Iterative Loop (from EvolveCoder's approach)
  For tasks flagged as weak:
  - Generate harder/more discriminative tests (using Claude, guided by specific
  failure modes found in step 2)
  - Re-run verifier scoring (step 1) on augmented task
  - Measure improvement
  - Repeat until verifier score plateaus
  This is the "hill climbing" the OAI lead wants.

  What's technically interesting about this combination

  - Verifier scoring uses the NVIDIA framework (principled metrics, not vibes)
  - Adversarial generation is iterative and execution-informed (not one-shot
  LLM)
  - Hybrid verification catches both false positives AND false negatives (not
  just hackability)
  - The loop converges — each iteration, verifier score measurably improves
  - Validated by training — Colab experiment proves the whole pipeline works

  What we'd implement from papers

  ┌────────────────┬──────────────────┬────────────────────────────────────┐
  │     Paper      │   What we take   │                How                 │
  ├────────────────┼──────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ Scoring        │ 4 verifier       │ Generate diverse patches, compute  │
  │ Verifiers      │ quality metrics  │ Top-1/Spearman/Bottom-1/MAE        │
  │ (NVIDIA)       │                  │                                    │
  ├────────────────┼──────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────┤

  │                │ refinement       │ tests based on execution           │
  ├────────────────┼──────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────┤
  │ R2E-Gym        │ Hybrid           │ Compare test results vs LLM judge  │

  ├────────────────┼──────────────────┼────────────────────────────────────┤

  - Verifier scoring is principled (NVIDIA metrics, not vibes)
